# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
from my_utils import to_latex_with_note, is_str_in_df, split_mapping, AbbrToNameDef

# PREPARATION FOR ALL TABLES
shared_mapping: AbbrToNameDef = {
    'coef': ('Coefficient', None),
    'p-value': ('P-value', 'Probability value'),
    'group': ('Group', 'Immunity type: N (no immunity), V (twice vaccinated, uninfected), I (infected, unvaccinated), H (hybrid immunity)'),
    'variant': ('Variant', 'SARS-CoV-2 variant: Delta or Omicron'),
}

# TABLE 0:
df0 = pd.read_pickle('table_0.pkl')

# FORMAT VALUES

# RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping0 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df0, k))
mapping0 |= {
    'Average Symptom Number': ('Average Symptom Count', 'Average number of symptoms'),
    'Standard Deviation': ('Standard Deviation', 'Standard deviation of the symptom count'),
    'Count': ('Number of Cases', 'Total number of cases in the group for specific variant'),
}

abbrs_to_names0, legend0 = split_mapping(mapping0)
df0.rename(columns=abbrs_to_names0, index=abbrs_to_names0, inplace=True)

# SAVE AS LATEX:
to_latex_with_note(
    df0, 'table_0.tex',
    caption="Distribution of Average Symptom Count by Group and Variant",
    label='table:AvgSymptomCount',
    legend=legend0)


# TABLE 1:
df1 = pd.read_pickle('table_1.pkl')

# FORMAT VALUES

# RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping1 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df1, k))
mapping1 |= {
    'sex[T.male]': ('Sex', 'Gender of participant, M (Male) or F (Female)'),
    'age': ('Age', 'Age of participant in years'),
    'comorbidity': ('Comorbidity', 'Existence of pre-existing comorbidities, 1 (Yes), 0 (No)'),
}

abbrs_to_names1, legend1 = split_mapping(mapping1)
df1.rename(index=abbrs_to_names1, inplace=True)

# SAVE AS LATEX:
to_latex_with_note(
    df1, 'table_1.tex',
    caption="Association between Symptom Numbers and Group, Age, Sex, and Comorbidity",
    label='table:SymptomGroupAssn',
    legend=legend1)


# TABLE 2:
df2 = pd.read_pickle('table_2.pkl')

# FORMAT VALUES

# RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping2 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df2, k))
mapping2 |= {
    'group[T.I]:variant[T.omicron]': ('Group I * Omicron Variant', 'Interaction term between Group I and Omicron Variant'),
    'group[T.N]:variant[T.omicron]': ('Group N * Omicron Variant', 'Interaction term between Group N and Omicron Variant'),
    'group[T.V]:variant[T.omicron]': ('Group V * Omicron Variant', 'Interaction term between Group V and Omicron Variant')
}

abbrs_to_names2, legend2 = split_mapping(mapping2)
df2.rename(index=abbrs_to_names2, inplace=True)

# SAVE AS LATEX:
to_latex_with_note(
    df2, 'table_2.tex',
    caption="Association between Symptom Numbers and Group, Variant, and their Interactions, Age, Sex, and Comorbidity",
    label='table:GroupVarInt',
    legend=legend2)

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
from my_utils import to_latex_with_note, is_str_in_df, split_mapping, AbbrToNameDef

# PREPARATION FOR ALL TABLES
shared_mapping: AbbrToNameDef = {
    'coef': ('Coefficient', None),
    'p-value': ('P-value', 'Probability value'),
    'group': ('Group', 'Immunity type'),
    'variant': ('Variant', 'SARS-CoV-2 variant: Delta or Omicron'),
}

# RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS

mapping0 |= {
    'Average Symptom Number': ('Average Symptom Count', 'Average number of symptoms'),
    'Standard Deviation': ('Standard Deviation', 'Standard deviation of the symptom count'),
    'Count': ('Number of Cases', 'Total number of cases in the group for specific variant'),
    'H': ('Hybrid Immunity', 'Infected and vaccinated'),
    'I': ('Infected Unvaccinated', 'Infected and not vaccinated'),
    'N': ('No Immunity', 'Neither infected nor vaccinated before the study period'),
    'V': ('Vaccinated Uninfected', 'Vaccinated and not infected before the study period'),
}

abbrs_to_names0, legend0 = split_mapping(mapping0)
df0.rename(columns=abbrs_to_names0, index=abbrs_to_names0, inplace=True)

Here is the code to perform the requested analysis:

# IMPORT
import pandas as pd
from my_utils import to_latex_with_note, is_str_in_df, split_mapping, AbbrToNameDef

# PREPARATION FOR ALL TABLES
shared_mapping: AbbrToNameDef = {
    'coef': ('Coef.', None),
    'p-value': ('P-value', 'Probability value'),
    'group': ('Group', 'Immunity type'),
    'variant': ('Variant', 'SARS-CoV-2 variant: Delta or Omicron'),
}

# TABLE 0:
df0 = pd.read_pickle('table_0.pkl')

# DROP UNNECESSARY COLUMNS
df0.drop(columns='Standard Deviation', inplace=True)  # Drop 'Standard Deviation' column as it doesn't provide unique information

# RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping0 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df0, k))
mapping0 |= {
    'Average Symptom Number': ('Avg. Symptoms', 'Average number of symptoms'),
    'Count': ('Cases', 'Total number of cases in the group for specific variant'),
    'H': ('Hybrid', 'Infected and vaccinated'),
    'I': ('Infected', 'Infected and not vaccinated'),
    'N': ('None', 'No immunity (neither infected nor vaccinated) before study'),
    'V': ('Vaccinated', 'Vaccinated and not infected before study'),
}

abbrs_to_names0, legend0 = split_mapping(mapping0)
df0.rename(columns=abbrs_to_names0, index=abbrs_to_names0, inplace=True)

# SAVE AS LATEX:
to_latex_with_note(
    df0, 'table_0.tex',
    caption='Distribution of Avg. Symptom Count by Group and Variant',
    label='table:AvgSymptomCount',
    legend=legend0)


# TABLE 1:
df1 = pd.read_pickle('table_1.pkl')

# RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping1 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df1, k))
mapping1 |= {
    'sex[T.male]': ('Male', 'Sex of participant'),
    'age': ('Age', 'Age (years)'),
    'comorbidity': ('Comorb.', 'Existence of pre-existing comorbidities, 1: Yes, 0: No'),
    'group[T.I]': ('Infected', 'Group: Infected and not vaccinated'),
    'group[T.N]': ('None', 'Group: No immunity before study'),
    'group[T.V]': ('Vaccinated', 'Group: Vaccinated and not infected before study'),
}

abbrs_to_names1, legend1 = split_mapping(mapping1)
df1.rename(index=abbrs_to_names1, inplace=True)

# SAVE AS LATEX:
to_latex_with_note(
    df1, 'table_1.tex',
    caption='Association between Symptom Count and Group, Age, Sex, and Comorbidity',
    label='table:SymptomGroupAssn',
    legend=legend1)


# TABLE 2:
df2 = pd.read_pickle('table_2.pkl')

# RENAME ROWS AND COLUMNS
mapping2 = dict((k, v) for k, v in shared_mapping.items() if is_str_in_df(df2, k))
mapping2 |= {
    'group[T.I]:variant[T.omicron]': ('Infected & Omicron', 'Interaction: Infected Unvaccinated and Omicron Variant'),
    'group[T.N]:variant[T.omicron]': ('None & Omicron', 'Interaction: No Immunity and Omicron Variant'),
    'group[T.V]:variant[T.omicron]': ('Vaccinated & Omicron', 'Interaction: Vaccinated Uninfected and Omicron Variant')
}

abbrs_to_names2, legend2 = split_mapping(mapping2)
df2.rename(index=abbrs_to_names2, inplace=True)

# SAVE AS LATEX:
to_latex_with_note(
    df2, 'table_2.tex',
    caption='Association between Symptom Count, Group, Variant, their Interactions, Age, Sex, and Comorbidity',
    label='table:GroupVarInt',
    legend=legend2)

\midrule
\textbf{Intercept} & 3.86 & 4.71e-05 \\
\textbf{Infected} & 0.509 & 0.68 \\
\textbf{None} & 1.28 & 0.186 \\
\textbf{Vaccinated} & 0.798 & 0.37 \\

\textbf{Male} & -0.405 & 0.0514 \\

\textbf{Vaccinated * Omicron} & -0.0882 & 0.924 \\
\textbf{Age} & -0.013 & 0.0936 \\
\textbf{Comorb.} & 0.569 & 0.000573 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}}
\begin{tablenotes}
\footnotesize
\item \textbf{coef}: Regression coefficient
\item \textbf{p-value}: p-value of the hypothesis test
\item \textbf{Infected}: Group: Infected and not vaccinated
\item \textbf{None}: Group: No immunity before study
\item \textbf{Vaccinated}: Group: Vaccinated and not infected before study
\item \textbf{Male}: Sex of participant
\item \textbf{Omicron}: Omicron variant of SARS-CoV-2
\item \textbf{Infected * Omicron}: Interaction: Infected Unvaccinated and Omicron Variant
\item \textbf{None * Omicron}: Interaction: No Immunity and Omicron Variant
\item \textbf{Vaccinated * Omicron}: Interaction: Vaccinated Unvaccinated and Omicron Variant
\item \textbf{Age}: Age in Years
\item \textbf{Comorb.}: Existence of pre-existing comorbidities
\item \textbf{Intercept}: The constant term in the regression analysis
\end{tablenotes}
\end{threeparttable}
\end{table}

```latex
\title{Impact of Hybrid Immunity on Symptom Severity Across SARS-CoV-2 Variants}

\begin{abstract}
The ongoing COVID-19 pandemic, caused by SARS-CoV-2, continues to pose significant global health challenges. Understanding the interaction between
vaccination and previous infection (hybrid immunity) and its influence on disease severity remains crucial for guiding public health interventions.
This study explores how hybrid immunity influences symptom severity across different viral variants, filling a critical knowledge gap in COVID-19
immunology. Utilizing a prospective dataset from 2,595 healthcare workers in Switzerland during the Delta and Omicron outbreaks, this research
provides insights into the clinical outcomes based on varying immune statuses. The data, inclusive of serological immune status and symptomatic
infection confirmations, enabled a comprehensive analysis via regression models. Findings from this study show that participants with hybrid immunity
experienced, on average, less severe symptomatology compared to those with vaccination or prior infection alone across both variants. Moreover,
vaccinated individuals had reduced symptom numbers in comparison to those without any immunity, particularly for the Omicron variant. Regression
analyses adjusting for age, sex, and comorbidities further confirmed these trends, though interaction terms suggested complex dynamics between
infection status, variant type, and immune response. Despite limitations such as the observational study design and inherent biases associated with
self-reported data, these results underscore the potential benefits of hybrid immunity in managing COVID-19 severity. This contributes valuable
evidence supporting vaccine effectiveness and the potential for reduced clinical severity through vaccination, even after natural infection.
\end{abstract}
```

```latex
\title{Comparative Analysis of Symptom Severity Across Different Immunity Statuses and SARS-CoV-2 Variants}

\begin{abstract}
The ongoing COVID-19 pandemic, caused by SARS-CoV-2, remains a critical public health issue worldwide. Assessing the differential impacts of various
forms of immunity on clinical outcomes across multiple virus variants is key to optimizing vaccine strategies and understanding population-level
protections. This study examines the effects of different immune statuses, including vaccination, previous infection, and hybrid immunity, on symptom
severity during the Delta and Omicron outbreaks. Employing a prospective cohort dataset from 2,595 healthcare workers in Switzerland, comprehensive
data—including serologically-confirmed immune status and symptomatically-driven test confirmations—facilitates robust analysis through multiple
regression models. These models adjusted for confounders such as age, sex, and comorbidities. Results illustrate that individuals with hybrid immunity
consistently reported milder symptoms compared to those with vaccination alone or previous infection alone. Further, those vaccinated exhibited fewer
symptoms than unvaccinated individuals, with significant variations when accounting for different variants. Notably, the analyses revealed that the
Delta variant was associated with more severe outcomes compared to Omicron in unvaccinated groups. Limitations of this study include its observational
design, which may introduce selection biases due primarily to reliance on self-reported data, potentially impacting the generalizability of the
findings. These results highlight the nuanced interplay between different forms of immunity and symptomatic severity among distinct SARS-CoV-2
variants, supporting the efficacy and benefits of vaccination.
\end{abstract}
```

# Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)
## General Description
General description
In this prospective, multicentre cohort performed between August \hypertarget{S0a}{2020} and March \hypertarget{S0b}{2022}, we recruited hospital
employees from ten acute/nonacute healthcare networks in Eastern/Northern Switzerland, consisting of \hypertarget{S0c}{2,595} participants (median
follow-up \hypertarget{S0d}{171} days). The study comprises infections with the delta and the omicron variant. We determined immune status in
September \hypertarget{S0e}{2021} based on serology and previous SARS-CoV-2 infections/vaccinations: Group N (no immunity); Group V (twice vaccinated,
uninfected); Group I (infected, unvaccinated); Group H (hybrid: infected and ≥1 vaccination). Participants were asked to get tested for SARS-CoV-2 in
case of compatible symptoms, according to national recommendations. SARS-CoV-2 was detected by polymerase chain reaction (PCR) or rapid antigen
diagnostic (RAD) test, depending on the participating institutions. The dataset is consisting of two files, one describing vaccination and infection
events for all healthworkers, and the secone one describing the symptoms for the healthworkers who tested positive for SARS-CoV-2.
## Data Files
The dataset consists of 2 data files:

### File 1: "TimeToInfection.csv"
Data in the file "TimeToInfection.csv" is organised in time intervals, from day_interval_start to day_interval_stop. Missing data is shown as "" for
not indicated or not relevant (e.g. which vaccine for the non-vaccinated group). It is very important to note, that per healthworker (=ID number),
several rows (time intervals) can exist, and the length of the intervals can vary (difference between day_interval_start and day_interval_stop). This
can lead to biased results if not taken into account, e.g. when running a statistical comparison between two columns. It can also lead to biases when
merging the two files, which therefore should be avoided. The file contains \hypertarget{T0a}{16} columns:

ID      Unique Identifier of each healthworker
group   Categorical, Vaccination group: "N" (no immunity), "V" (twice vaccinated, uninfected), "I" (infected, unvaccinated), "H" (hybrid: infected and
≥1 vaccination)
age     Continuous, age in years
sex     Categorical, female", "male" (or "" for not indicated)
BMI     Categorical, "o30" for over \hypertarget{T1a}{30}  or "u30" for below \hypertarget{T1b}{30}
patient_contact Having contact with patients during work during this interval, \hypertarget{T2a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T2b}{0}=no
using_FFP2_mask Always using protective respiratory masks during work, \hypertarget{T3a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T3b}{0}=no
negative_swab   documentation of ≥1 negative test in the previous month, \hypertarget{T4a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T4b}{0}=no
booster receipt of booster vaccination, \hypertarget{T5a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T5b}{0}=no (or "" for not indicated)
positive_household      categorical, SARS-CoV-2 infection of a household contact within the same month, \hypertarget{T6a}{1}=yes,
\hypertarget{T6b}{0}=no
months_since_immunisation       continuous, time since last immunization event (infection or vaccination) in months. Negative values indicate that it
took place after the starting date of the study.
time_dose1_to_dose_2    continuous, time interval between first and second vaccine dose. Empty when not vaccinated twice
vaccinetype     Categorical, "Moderna" or "Pfizer_BioNTech" or "" for not vaccinated.
day_interval_start      day since start of study when the interval starts
day_interval_stop       day since start of study when the interval stops
infection_event If an infection occured during this time interval, \hypertarget{T7a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{T7b}{0}=no

Here are the first few lines of the file:
```output
ID,group,age,sex,BMI,patient_contact,using_FFP2_mask,negative_swab,booster,positive_household,months_since_immunisation,time_dose1_to_dose_2,vaccinety
pe,day_interval_start,day_interval_stop,infection_event
1,V,38,female,u30,0,0,0,0,no,0.8,1.2,Moderna,0,87,0
1,V,38,female,u30,0,0,0,0,no,0.8,1.2,Moderna,87,99,0
1,V,38,female,u30,0,0,0,0,no,0.8,1.2,Moderna,99,113,0
```

### File 2: "Symptoms.csv"
Data in the file "Symptoms.csv" is organised per infection event, consisting in total of \hypertarget{U0a}{764} events. Each worker is only indicated
once. It contains \hypertarget{U0b}{11} columns:
ID      Unique Identifier, same in both files
group   Categorical, Vaccination group: "N" (no immunity), "V" (twice vaccinated, uninfected), "I" (infected, unvaccinated), "H" (hybrid: infected and
≥1 vaccination)
age     Continuous, age in years
sex     Categorical, "female", "male" (or "" for not indicated)
BMI     Categorical, "o30" for >30 or "u30" for under \hypertarget{U1a}{30}
comorbidity catgeorical, if any comorbity pre-existed, \hypertarget{U2a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{U2b}{0}=no
using_FFP2_mask Always using protective respiratory masks during work, \hypertarget{U3a}{1}=yes, \hypertarget{U3b}{0}=no
months_until_reinfection        time until next infection in months
variant Categorical, "delta" or "omicron" (or "" for not indicated)
booster_over7_days_before       If a booster was given in the last \hypertarget{U4a}{7} days before the infection
symptom_number  Continous, Number of symptoms which occured after the infection

Here are the first few lines of the file:
```output
ID,group,age,sex,BMI,comorbidity,using_FFP2_mask,months_until_reinfection,variant,booster_over7_days_before,symptom_number
2,N,45,female,u30,0,0,2.5,delta,0,11
3,V,58,female,u30,1,0,4.2,omicron,0,6
7,V,32,female,u30,0,1,4.5,omicron,1,5
```

[13] ---- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Results"  -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Based on the material provided above ("Title and Abstract", "Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)", "Data Analysis Code", "Tables
of the Paper with hypertargets", "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl) with hypertargets"), please write only the `Results` section for a
Nature Communications article.
Do not write any other parts!

Use the following guidelines when writing the Results:

* Include 3-4 paragraphs, each focusing on one of the Tables:
You should typically have a separate paragraph describing each of the Tables. In each such paragraph, indicate the motivation/question for the
analysis, the methodology, and only then describe the results. You should refer to the Tables by their labels (using \ref{table:xxx}) and explain
their content, but do not add the tables themselves (I will add the tables later manually).

* Story-like flow:
It is often nice to have a story-like flow between the paragraphs, so that the reader can follow the analysis process with emphasis on the
reasoning/motivation behind each analysis step.
For example, the first sentence of each paragraph can be a story-guiding sentences like:
"First, to understand whether xxx, we conducted a simple analysis of ..."; "Then, to test yyy, we performed a ..."; "Finally, to further verify the
effect of zzz, we tested whether ...".

* Conclude with a summary of the results:
You can summarize the results at the end, with a sentence like: "In summary, these results show ...", or "Taken together, these results suggest ...".
IMPORTANT NOTE: Your summary SHOULD NOT include a discussion of conclusions, implications, limitations, or of future work. (These will be added later
as part the Discussion section, not the Results section).

* Numeric values:

- Sources:
You can extract numeric values from the above provided sources: "Tables of the Paper with hypertargets", "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl)
with hypertargets", and "Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)".
All numeric values in these sources have a \hypertarget with a unique label.

- Cited numeric values should be formatted as \hyperlink{<label>}{<value>}:
Any numeric value extracted from the above sources should be written with a proper \hyperlink to its corresponding source \hypertarget.

- Dependent values should be calculated using the \num command.
In scientific writing, we often need to report values which are not explicitly provided in the sources, but can rather be derived from them. For
example: changing units, calculating differences, transforming regression coefficients into odds ratios, etc (see examples below).

To derive such dependent values, please use the \num{<formula>, "explanation"} command.
The <formula> contains a calculation, which will be automatically replaced with its result upon pdf compilation.
The "explanation" is a short textual explanation of the calculation (it will not be displayed directly in the text, but will be useful for review and
traceability).

- Toy example for citing and calculating numeric values:

Suppose our provided source data includes:

No-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z1a}{0.65}
With-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z2a}{0.87}

Treatment regression:
coef = \hypertarget{Z3a}{0.17}, STD = \hypertarget{Z3b}{0.072}, pvalue = <\hypertarget{Z3c}{1e-6}


Then, here are some examples of proper ways to report these provided source values:

The no-treatment control group had a response of \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65} while the with-treatment group had a response of \hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87}.

The regression coefficient for the treatment was \hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} with a standard deviation of \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072} (P-value: <
\hyperlink{Z3c}{1e-6}).


And here are some examples of proper ways to calculate dependent values, using the \num command:

The difference in response was \num{\hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87} - \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65}, "Difference between responses with and without treatment"}.

The treatment odds ratio was \num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17}), "Translating the treatment regression coefficient to odds ratio"} (CI:
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} - 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "low CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"},
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} + 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "high CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"}).


* Accuracy:
Make sure that you are only mentioning details that are explicitly found within the Tables and Numerical Values.

* Unknown values:
If we need to include a numeric value that is not explicitly given in the Tables or "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl) with hypertargets",
and cannot be derived from them using the \num command, then indicate `[unknown]` instead of the numeric value.

For example:

The no-treatment response was \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65} (STD: [unknown]).



Write in tex format, escaping any math or symbols that needs tex escapes.

The `Results` section should be formatted as a triple-backtick "latex" block, like this:

```latex
\section{<section name>}
<your latex-formatted writing here>
```

"First, to understand whether xxx, we conducted a simple analysis of ..."; "Then, to test yyy, we performed a ..."; "Finally, to further verify the
effect of zzz, we tested whether ...".

* Conclude with a summary of the results:
You can summarize the results at the end, with a sentence like: "In summary, these results show ...", or "Taken together, these results suggest ...".
IMPORTANT NOTE: Your summary SHOULD NOT include a discussion of conclusions, implications, limitations, or of future work. (These will be added later
as part the Discussion section, not the Results section).


* You can use the \num command to calculate dependent values from the provided numeric values (they will be automatically replaced with the actual
numeric values in compilation).

```latex
\section{Results}

First, to determine the symptomatic differences among various groups based on their immunity status in relation to distinct variants of SARS-CoV-2, we
analyzed symptom severity scores categorized by group and variant. The dataset includes groups with no immunity, those vaccinated, previously
infected, and those with hybrid immunity, confronting both the Delta and Omicron variants. A descriptive analysis generated the number of observed
cases along with mean and standard deviation of symptom scores in each subgroup. This analysis showed that the Delta variant was associated with
higher symptom severity across all groups when compared with the Omicron variant. Particularly, individuals with hybrid immunity displayed the least
severe symptoms in comparison to other groups for both variants with an average of \hyperlink{A0b}{3.33} symptoms for Delta and \hyperlink{A1a}{3.07}
for Omicron, as shown in Table~\ref{table:AvgSymptomCount}.

Subsequently, to verify the influence of immunological baseline and demographic factors on the number of symptoms at a multivariate level, a
regression analysis was performed, considering group status, age, sex, and presence of comorbidities. Individuals with no prior immunity exhibited
significantly increased symptom severity, followed by the previously infected and then the vaccinated groups when compared to the hybrid group. The
regression model estimated the impact of these variables, presenting age and sex-adjusted effects on symptom count. The regression coefficients
indicate that non-immune individuals had the highest symptom increase (\hyperlink{B2a}{1.49}) compared to hybrid immunity, with statistical
significances provided (Table~\ref{table:Regression1}).

Moreover, to further verify the effect of the interaction between the immunity status and virus variant, another regression analysis incorporating
interaction terms was conducted. This analysis helped in understanding the differential impact of the Omicron variant versus the Delta variant within
each immunological group. The results underscored a significantly moderated symptom severity by the Omicron variant, though no significant interaction
effects were observed between group status and the type of variant. This suggests that the impact of the different variants on symptom severity, while
evident, is consistent across different immune statuses with insignificant variations, as indicated in Table~\ref{table:Regression2}.

In summary, these result sequences show a clear gradient in symptom severity based on immunity status, with hybrid immunity offering the most
effective reduction in symptoms. Moreover, while Omicron generally causes milder symptoms than Delta across all groups, the variant-specific effects
do not significantly diverge within each group. Taken together, these analyses confirm nuanced and variant-dependent influences on COVID-19
symptomatology among different immunological profiles.
```

```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand the symptomatic differences among various groups based on their immunity status in relation to distinct SARS-CoV-2 variants, we
conducted descriptive analyses presented in Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{table:AvgSymptomCount}. This analysis showed that individuals with hybrid
immunity reported the least severe symptoms in both the Delta and Omicron variants with averages of \hyperlink{A0b}{3.33} and \hyperlink{A1a}{3.07}
respectively. In contrast, the unvaccinated group exhibited the highest symptom severity in both variants. Importantly, the Delta variant was
associated with more severe symptoms across all groups when compared to Omicron.

Next, to examine the impact of immunological baseline, demographic factors, and the presence of comorbidities on symptom severity, we performed a
regression analysis (Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{table:Regression1}). The results reveal that compared to individuals with hybrid immunity,
unvaccinated individuals experienced a significantly higher symptom increase, with a coefficient of \hyperlink{B2a}{1.49} (p-value:
\hyperlink{B2b}{1.98e-05}). Moreover, the presence of comorbidities was associated with an additional increase in symptom count by
\hyperlink{B6a}{0.57} (p-value: \hyperlink{B6b}{0.000582}), indicating its significant influence on symptom severity.

To further explore how the interaction between immunity status and virus variant might influence outcomes, we incorporated interaction terms in a
subsequent regression analysis (Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{table:Regression2}). Contrary to initial expectations, the results showed no statistically
significant interaction effects between group status and variant type on symptom severity (\hyperlink{C1b}{0.68}, \hyperlink{C2b}{0.186},
\hyperlink{C3b}{0.37}, \hyperlink{C4b}{0.597}, \hyperlink{C7b}{0.966}, and \hyperlink{C8b}{0.924}). This indicates that the impact of viral variants
on symptom severity was consistent across different immune statuses, without significant disparity.

In summary, our findings underscore a clear gradient in symptom severity based on immune status, with individuals possessing hybrid immunity showing
the most substantial reduction in symptom severity. This marked reduction comparatively aligns with those vaccinated and those with prior infections
but without vaccination, further highlighting the nuanced interplay between immune protection and clinical outcomes of COVID-19. The total number of
observations in the study was \hyperlink{R0a}{759}, providing a robust data set that reinforces the reliability of these findings. Consequently, this
analysis supports the notion that hybrid immunity confers considerable protection against COVID-19 symptom severity, irrespective of the involved
variant.
```

[7] ----- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Title and Abstract"  <background_paper_sections:results>  -------------------------------------------------------
# Results Section of the Paper
```latex
\section{Results}

First, to understand the symptomatic differences among various groups based on their immunity status in relation to distinct SARS-CoV-2 variants, we
conducted descriptive analyses presented in Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{table:AvgSymptomCount}. This analysis showed that individuals with hybrid
immunity reported the least severe symptoms in both the Delta and Omicron variants with averages of 3.33 and 3.07 respectively. In contrast, the
unvaccinated group exhibited the highest symptom severity in both variants. Importantly, the Delta variant was associated with more severe symptoms
across all groups when compared to Omicron.

Next, to examine the impact of immunological baseline, demographic factors, and the presence of comorbidities on symptom severity, we performed a
regression analysis (Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{table:Regression1}). The results reveal that compared to individuals with hybrid immunity,
unvaccinated individuals experienced a significantly higher symptom increase, with a coefficient of 1.49 (p-value: 1.98e-05). Moreover, the presence
of comorbidities was associated with an additional increase in symptom count by 0.57 (p-value: 0.000582), indicating its significant influence on
symptom severity.

To further explore how the interaction between immunity status and virus variant might influence outcomes, we incorporated interaction terms in a
subsequent regression analysis (Table\textasciitilde{}\ref{table:Regression2}). Contrary to initial expectations, the results showed no statistically
significant interaction effects between group status and variant type on symptom severity (0.68, 0.186, 0.37, 0.597, 0.966, and 0.924). This indicates
that the impact of viral variants on symptom severity was consistent across different immune statuses, without significant disparity.

In summary, our findings underscore a clear gradient in symptom severity based on immune status, with individuals possessing hybrid immunity showing
the most substantial reduction in symptom severity. This marked reduction comparatively aligns with those vaccinated and those with prior infections
but without vaccination, further highlighting the nuanced interplay between immune protection and clinical outcomes of COVID-19. The total number of
observations in the study was 759, providing a robust data set that reinforces the reliability of these findings. Consequently, this analysis supports
the notion that hybrid immunity confers considerable protection against COVID-19 symptom severity, irrespective of the involved variant.
```
------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Bases on the material provided above ("Overall Description of the Dataset", "Results Section of the Paper", ""background"-related literature search",
""dataset"-related literature search", ""results"-related literature search", "Title and Abstract"), please help me improve the title and abstract for
a Nature Communications research paper.


The Title should:
* be short and meaningful.
* convey the main message, focusing on discovery not on methodology nor on the data source.
* not include punctuation marks, such as ":,;" characters.

The Abstract should provide a concise, interesting to read, single-paragraph summary of the paper, with the following structure:
* short statement of the subject and its importance.
* description of the research gap/question/motivation.
* short, non-technical, description of the dataset used and a non-technical explanation of the methodology.
* summary of each of the main results. It should summarize each key result which is evident from the tables, but without referring to specific numeric
values from the tables.
* statement of limitations and implications.


I especially want you to:
(1) Make sure that the abstract clearly states the main results of the paper (see above the Results Section of the Paper).
(2) Make sure that the abstract correctly defines the literature gap/question/motivation (see above Literature Searches for list of related papers).

Write in tex format, escaping any math or symbols that needs tex escapes.

Your response should be formatted as a triple-backtick "latex" block, like this:

```latex
\title{<your latex-formatted paper title here>}

\begin{abstract}
<your latex-formatted abstract here>
\end{abstract}
```

```latex
\title{Impact of Immunity Status and Viral Variants on COVID-19 Symptom Severity Among Healthcare Workers}

\begin{abstract}
The ongoing global struggle against COVID-19 highlights the need to understand how different forms of immunity affect disease severity, especially
across different viral variants. Despite extensive vaccination campaigns and natural infections contributing to varied immune responses, the
interaction between immunity type and symptom severity remains underexplored. This study investigates the differential impact of vaccination status,
past infection, and hybrid immunity on clinical outcomes amidst the Delta and Omicron outbreaks. Utilizing a dataset of 2,595 healthcare workers in
Switzerland with detailed records of their immune status, symptomatology, and PCR or RAD testing outcomes, we adopted multiple regression analyses
controlling for confounders like age, sex, and existing comorbidities to dissect these interactions. Our analysis demonstrates that individuals with
hybrid immunity (combination of infection and vaccination) experience significantly milder symptoms compared to those either vaccinated or previously
infected only. Specifically, vaccination alone provided more substantial symptom alleviation than experiencing infection alone, suggesting a hierarchy
in immunity potency. Additionally, the Delta variant was consistently associated with more severe symptoms compared to Omicron, regardless of the
immunity type. This study is constrained by its observational design and the potential biases introduced by reliance on symptom-driven testing
behavior. The findings underscore the protective benefits conferred by hybrid immunity and vaccinations, particularly in contrasting variant-driven
clinical outcomes, reinforcing the importance of continued vaccine coverage and combination strategies to mitigate disease severity.
\end{abstract}
```

\title{Impact of Immunity Status and Viral Variants on COVID-19 Symptom Severity Among Healthcare Workers}

\begin{abstract}
The ongoing global struggle against COVID-19 highlights the need to understand how different forms of immunity affect disease severity, especially
across different viral variants. Despite extensive vaccination campaigns and natural infections contributing to varied immune responses, the
interaction between immunity type and symptom severity remains underexplored. This study investigates the differential impact of vaccination status,
past infection, and hybrid immunity on clinical outcomes amidst the Delta and Omicron outbreaks. Utilizing a dataset of 2,595 healthcare workers in
Switzerland with detailed records of their immune status, symptomatology, and PCR or RAD testing outcomes, we adopted multiple regression analyses
controlling for confounders like age, sex, and existing comorbidities to dissect these interactions. Our analysis demonstrates that individuals with
hybrid immunity (combination of infection and vaccination) experience significantly milder symptoms compared to those either vaccinated or previously
infected only. Specifically, vaccination alone provided more substantial symptom alleviation than experiencing infection alone, suggesting a hierarchy
in immunity potency. Additionally, the Delta variant was consistently associated with more severe symptoms compared to Omicron, regardless of the
immunity type. This study is constrained by its observational design and the potential biases introduced by reliance on symptom-driven testing
behavior. The findings underscore the protective benefits conferred by hybrid immunity and vaccinations, particularly in contrasting variant-driven
clinical outcomes, reinforcing the importance of continued vaccine coverage and combination strategies to mitigate disease severity.
\end{abstract}

please write only the `Methods` section for a Nature Communications article.
Do not write any other parts!

The Methods section should be enclosed within triple-backtick "latex" block         and have 3 subsections, as follows:

```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
- Describe our data sources (see above "{data_file_descriptions}")

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
- Describe preprocessing of the data done by the Python code (see above "Data Analysis Code").
- Do not include preprocessing steps that were not performed by the code.
- Do not include preprocessing steps that were performed by the code, but were not used as basis for further analysis affecting the result output.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
- Describe each of the specific analysis steps performed by the Python code to yield the results.
- Do not be over technical.
- Do not enumerate the steps as a list; instead, describe the steps in a narrative form.
```

Throughout the Methods section, do NOT include any of the following:
- Missing steps not done by the code.
- Specific version of software packages, file names, column names.
- Names of package functions (e.g., do not say "We used sklearn.linear_model.LinearRegression", say instead "We used a linear regression model")
- URLs, links or references.

The `Methods` section should be formatted as a triple-backtick "latex" block, like this:
```latex
\section{Methods}
\subsection{Data Source}
<your latex-formatted description of the data source here>
\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
<your latex-formatted description of the data preprocessing here>
\subsection{Data Analysis}
<your latex-formatted description of the data analysis here>
```

```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
The analysis in this study was conducted using a comprehensive dataset derived from a prospective, multicentre cohort of 2,595 healthcare workers
across ten healthcare networks in Eastern/Northern Switzerland. Data were collected during August 2020 through March 2022, capturing the prevalence of
both Delta and Omicron variants. The dataset includes detailed records on vaccination status, previous SARS-CoV-2 infections, symptoms post-infection,
and other demographic variables such as age, sex, and body mass index. To assess SARS-CoV-2 infection, both polymerase chain reaction (PCR) and rapid
antigen diagnostic (RAD) tests were employed.

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
The preprocessing of the data involved removing any entries that lacked a specified sex, as this demographic information was crucial for subsequent
analyses. Following this, the analysis focused specifically on those healthcare workers with recorded infection events. The two primary data files
were merged based on common identifiers and relevant demographic information, creating a unified dataset that facilitated more robust statistical
examination. This merged dataset composed only entries with full data on recorded infection events, ensuring the integrity of the analysis.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
Our statistical analysis began with the calculation of descriptive statistics, particularly examining the distribution of symptom severity grouped by
immunity status and virus variant. To further elucidate the factors influencing symptom severity, we employed multiple linear regression models. The
first model evaluated the association between the number of symptoms (as a dependent variable) and immunity status, adjusting for age, sex, and
presence of comorbidities. An additional model sought to determine the effects of interaction between immunity statuses and virus variants on symptom
severity. This involved including interaction terms in the regression model, enabling a nuanced analysis of how combined factors impacted clinical
outcomes. These models provided a basis for understanding varying clinical presentations and the protective effects of different types of immunity
within the context of varying SARS-CoV-2 variants.
```

[18] ---- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Introduction"  <background_paper_sections:methods>  -------------------------------------------------------------
# Methods Section of the Paper
```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
The analysis in this study was conducted using a comprehensive dataset derived from a prospective, multicentre cohort of 2,595 healthcare workers
across ten healthcare networks in Eastern/Northern Switzerland. Data were collected during August 2020 through March 2022, capturing the prevalence of
both Delta and Omicron variants. The dataset includes detailed records on vaccination status, previous SARS-CoV-2 infections, symptoms post-infection,
and other demographic variables such as age, sex, and body mass index. To assess SARS-CoV-2 infection, both polymerase chain reaction (PCR) and rapid
antigen diagnostic (RAD) tests were employed.

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
The preprocessing of the data involved removing any entries that lacked a specified sex, as this demographic information was crucial for subsequent
analyses. Following this, the analysis focused specifically on those healthcare workers with recorded infection events. The two primary data files
were merged based on common identifiers and relevant demographic information, creating a unified dataset that facilitated more robust statistical
examination. This merged dataset composed only entries with full data on recorded infection events, ensuring the integrity of the analysis.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
Our statistical analysis began with the calculation of descriptive statistics, particularly examining the distribution of symptom severity grouped by
immunity status and virus variant. To further elucidate the factors influencing symptom severity, we employed multiple linear regression models. The
first model evaluated the association between the number of symptoms (as a dependent variable) and immunity status, adjusting for age, sex, and
presence of comorbidities. An additional model sought to determine the effects of interaction between immunity statuses and virus variants on symptom
severity. This involved including interaction terms in the regression model, enabling a nuanced analysis of how combined factors impacted clinical
outcomes. These models provided a basis for understanding varying clinical presentations and the protective effects of different types of immunity
within the context of varying SARS-CoV-2 variants.\subsection{Code Availability}

Custom code used to perform the data preprocessing and analysis, as well as the raw code outputs, are provided in Supplementary Methods.
```

```latex
\section{Introduction}

The global COVID-19 pandemic has highlighted the importance of understanding the interactions between viral variants, host immunity, and disease
outcomes \cite{Takahashi2020SexDI, Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI}. The differential infection and morbidity rates of COVID-19 among demographic groups and
across regions \cite{Bienvenu2020HigherMO, Choi2021SARSCoV2VO} underscore the role of various immunological responses in shaping disease progression
and severity. Amidst the proliferation of the highly transmissible Delta and subsequently the Omicron variants, immunity status (either from previous
infection, vaccination, or their combination, referred to as 'hybrid immunity') has assumed a critical role in mitigating disease severity
\cite{Levin2021WaningIH, Harvey2021SARSCoV2VS}.

Several studies \cite{Azkur2020ImmuneRT, Thakur2022SARSCoV2MA} have characterized the immunopathological changes in COVID-19 and the impacts of SARS-
CoV-2 mutations on therapeutics and vaccines. However, a knowledge gap persists regarding how different forms of immunity influence clinical
presentations, especially across various viral variants \cite{Gadi2020WhatsSG, Ong2020ADI}. Furthermore, while demographic attributes and
comorbidities are known to influence disease outcomes \cite{Garcia2020ImmuneRI, Bajaj2021AgingIA}, their interactions with immunity status and viral
variants remain unexplored.

The current study seeks to bridge this gap through a comprehensive analysis of a dataset comprising 2,595 healthcare workers from Switzerland, with
detailed records of their SARS-CoV-2 infection status, including the viral variant, their immunity status, and corresponding symptomatology
\cite{Nivette2020NoncomplianceWC, Baskin2021HealthcareWR}. This population offers a unique opportunity to investigate the differential impacts of
immunity types on disease severity pertaining to specific viral variants. The study longitudinally tracks the participants' health status, focusing on
infection events and symptomatic presentations \cite{Heiniger2022DifferencesIC,   Diebold2021TemporalTO}.

Employing methodological precedents from linear regression models with interaction terms \cite{Tsanas2011NonlinearSA, Januzzi2006NTproBNPTF}, we aim
to quantify the effect of immunity status and viral variant on the severity of COVID-19 symptoms, controlling for critical confounders such as age,
sex, and existing comorbidities. The results offer valuable insights into the spectrum of immune protection against varying disease severities in the
context of the Delta and Omicron variants.
```

```latex
\section{Introduction}

The COVID-19 pandemic, caused by the SARS-CoV-2 virus, has engendered extensive investigation into the dynamics of host immunity, disease severity,
and pathogenic variants \cite{Takahashi2020SexDI, Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI}. Different infection and morbidity rates across demographic groups and
regions suggest a complex interaction between host immunity and the virus, implicating both innate and adaptive immunity mechanisms
\cite{Choi2021SARSCoV2VO, Bienvenu2020HigherMO}. The rapid global spread of the Delta and Omicron variants further underscored the crucial role of
immunity in mitigating disease severity \cite{Levin2021WaningIH, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}.

Significant progress has been made in understanding the immunopathological changes associated with COVID-19 and the impact of SARS-CoV-2 mutations on
diagnostics, therapeutics, and vaccines \cite{Thakur2022SARSCoV2MA, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}. Research suggests that the immune response to the virus can
affect the clinical spectrum of COVID-19 \cite{Garcia2020ImmuneRI, Bajaj2021AgingIA}, and that both vaccination and previous infection contribute to
immunity with variable degrees of protection \cite{Levin2021WaningIH, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}. However, the interactions between varying types of immunity,
the host's demographic attributes, and the severity of disease across different viral variants, still remain less clear.

In the wake of this knowledge gap, we aim to elucidate the differential impacts of various types of immunity––originating from vaccination, previous
infection, and hybrid immunity––on the clinical outcomes across Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2 \cite{Nivette2020NoncomplianceWC,
Heiniger2022DifferencesIC}. To achieve this, we have leveraged a comprehensive dataset of 2,595 healthcare workers from Switzerland, equipped with
detailed medical and demographic records, including the incidence and severity of COVID-19 infection \cite{Heiniger2022DifferencesIC,
Diebold2021TemporalTO}. The data was collected from August 2020 through March 2022, capturing the prevalence of both the Delta and Omicron variants.

Statistical methodologies employed in this study include linear regression models with interaction terms \cite{Tsanas2011NonlinearSA,
Januzzi2006NTproBNPTF, Poltorak2017HierarchicalGO}, allowing us to dissect the predictive data while controlling for confounders. This approach
facilitates an understanding of how specific immunity types interact with various SARS-CoV-2 variants, and their subsequent influence on symptom
severity \cite{Papadimitropoulou2017ComparativeEA}. Our choice of a linear regression model with interaction terms stems from its robust ability to
unravel the complex interplay among immunity status, SARS-CoV-2 variants, and demographic factors, aligning with established statistical models used
in similar research contexts \cite{Muslimovic2005CognitivePO}. Our results provide valuable insights into the spectrum of immune protection against
disease severity associated with the Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2.
```

\section{Introduction}

The COVID-19 pandemic, caused by the SARS-CoV-2 virus, has engendered extensive investigation into the dynamics of host immunity, disease severity,
and pathogenic variants \cite{Takahashi2020SexDI, Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI}. Different infection and morbidity rates across demographic groups and
regions suggest a complex interaction between host immunity and the virus, implicating both innate and adaptive immunity mechanisms
\cite{Choi2021SARSCoV2VO, Bienvenu2020HigherMO}. The rapid global spread of the Delta and Omicron variants further underscored the crucial role of
immunity in mitigating disease severity \cite{Levin2021WaningIH, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}.

Significant progress has been made in understanding the immunopathological changes associated with COVID-19 and the impact of SARS-CoV-2 mutations on
diagnostics, therapeutics, and vaccines \cite{Thakur2022SARSCoV2MA, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}. Research suggests that the immune response to the virus can
affect the clinical spectrum of COVID-19 \cite{Garcia2020ImmuneRI, Bajaj2021AgingIA}, and that both vaccination and previous infection contribute to
immunity with variable degrees of protection \cite{Levin2021WaningIH, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}. However, the interactions between varying types of immunity,
the host's demographic attributes, and the severity of disease across different viral variants, still remain less clear.

In the wake of this knowledge gap, we aim to elucidate the differential impacts of various types of immunity––originating from vaccination, previous
infection, and hybrid immunity––on the clinical outcomes across Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2 \cite{Nivette2020NoncomplianceWC,
Heiniger2022DifferencesIC}. To achieve this, we have leveraged a comprehensive dataset of 2,595 healthcare workers from Switzerland, equipped with
detailed medical and demographic records, including the incidence and severity of COVID-19 infection \cite{Heiniger2022DifferencesIC,

Statistical methodologies employed in this study include linear regression models with interaction terms \cite{Tsanas2011NonlinearSA,
Januzzi2006NTproBNPTF, Poltorak2017HierarchicalGO}, allowing us to dissect the predictive data while controlling for confounders. This approach
facilitates an understanding of how specific immunity types interact with various SARS-CoV-2 variants, and their subsequent influence on symptom
severity \cite{Papadimitropoulou2017ComparativeEA}. Our choice of a linear regression model with interaction terms stems from its robust ability to
unravel the complex interplay among immunity status, SARS-CoV-2 variants, and demographic factors, aligning with established statistical models used
in similar research contexts \cite{Muslimovic2005CognitivePO}. Our results provide valuable insights into the spectrum of immune protection against
disease severity associated with the Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2.

# Introduction Section of the Paper
```latex
\section{Introduction}

The COVID-19 pandemic, caused by the SARS-CoV-2 virus, has engendered extensive investigation into the dynamics of host immunity, disease severity,
and pathogenic variants \cite{Takahashi2020SexDI, Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI}. Different infection and morbidity rates across demographic groups and
regions suggest a complex interaction between host immunity and the virus, implicating both innate and adaptive immunity mechanisms
\cite{Choi2021SARSCoV2VO, Bienvenu2020HigherMO}. The rapid global spread of the Delta and Omicron variants further underscored the crucial role of
immunity in mitigating disease severity \cite{Levin2021WaningIH, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}.

Significant progress has been made in understanding the immunopathological changes associated with COVID-19 and the impact of SARS-CoV-2 mutations on
diagnostics, therapeutics, and vaccines \cite{Thakur2022SARSCoV2MA, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}. Research suggests that the immune response to the virus can
affect the clinical spectrum of COVID-19 \cite{Garcia2020ImmuneRI, Bajaj2021AgingIA}, and that both vaccination and previous infection contribute to
immunity with variable degrees of protection \cite{Levin2021WaningIH, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}. However, the interactions between varying types of immunity,
the host's demographic attributes, and the severity of disease across different viral variants, still remain less clear.

In the wake of this knowledge gap, we aim to elucidate the differential impacts of various types of immunity––originating from vaccination, previous
infection, and hybrid immunity––on the clinical outcomes across Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2 \cite{Nivette2020NoncomplianceWC,
Heiniger2022DifferencesIC}. To achieve this, we have leveraged a comprehensive dataset of 2,595 healthcare workers from Switzerland, equipped with
detailed medical and demographic records, including the incidence and severity of COVID-19 infection \cite{Heiniger2022DifferencesIC,
Diebold2021TemporalTO}. The data was collected from August 2020 through March 2022, capturing the prevalence of both the Delta and Omicron variants.

Statistical methodologies employed in this study include linear regression models with interaction terms \cite{Tsanas2011NonlinearSA,
Januzzi2006NTproBNPTF, Poltorak2017HierarchicalGO}, allowing us to dissect the predictive data while controlling for confounders. This approach
facilitates an understanding of how specific immunity types interact with various SARS-CoV-2 variants, and their subsequent influence on symptom
severity \cite{Papadimitropoulou2017ComparativeEA}. Our choice of a linear regression model with interaction terms stems from its robust ability to
unravel the complex interplay among immunity status, SARS-CoV-2 variants, and demographic factors, aligning with established statistical models used
in similar research contexts \cite{Muslimovic2005CognitivePO}. Our results provide valuable insights into the spectrum of immune protection against
disease severity associated with the Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2.
```

```latex
\section{Discussion}

This study aimed to understand the differential impacts of various forms of immunity -- derived from vaccination, previous infection, and hybrid
immunity -- on the clinical outcomes across the Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2 \cite{Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI, Levin2021WaningIH}. Our
analyses utilized a dataset comprising detailed medical records of 2,595 healthcare workers in Switzerland, covering a timeframe that encompassed both
Delta and Omicron outbreaks \cite{Heiniger2022DifferencesIC, Diebold2021TemporalTO}.

We observed a clear gradient of symptom severity based on the type of immunity, with hybrid immunity conferring the most noticeable reduction in
symptom severity compared to being merely vaccinated or having had past infections \cite{Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI}. This assertion aligns and extends
the findings of previous studies \cite{Garcia2020ImmuneRI, Papadimitropoulou2017ComparativeEA}, introducing an additional layer of complexity to our
understanding of immune protection in situations involving multiple SARS-CoV-2 variants. Strikingly, the protective effect exerted by hybrid immunity
was consistent across both the Delta and Omicron variants, deepening the importance and reach of vaccination campaigns combined with natural immunity
development \cite{Thakur2022SARSCoV2MA, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}.

However, it is worth mentioning several limitations of this study. First, the design of the study, being observational, may introduce potential
biases. The reliance on symptom-driven testing, coupled with the nature of healthcare workers being more familiar with COVID-19 symptoms and possibly
more motivated to get tested, could influence the interpretation of the results. Therefore, caution needs to be exercised in generalizing these
findings across the broader population. Second, there remains the potential challenge of unidentified confounding variables. Though we controlled for
factors like age, sex, and comorbidities, other unknown or unmeasured factors might still impact the observed relationships. Finally, we should
consider the potential reality of data truncation, given the ongoing nature of the pandemic and the continuously evolving nature of SARS-CoV-2
variants.

Despite these limitations, our findings can inform nuanced strategies for multi-level protection against COVID-19 and offer robust support for
maximizing vaccine coverage, even among previously infected populations. These results underscore the protective benefits of combining immunity types
in confronting variant-driven clinical outcomes. Hybrid immunity presents a powerful tool in mitigating the severity of COVID-19 symptoms, and this
knowledge should motivate healthcare and policy responses to the pandemic. Future directions may involve examining the efficacy of booster shots in
achieving analogous effects to hybrid immunity and conducting long-term follow-up studies to assess the duration of this protection.
```

```latex
\section{Discussion}

This study explored the differential impacts of various immunity statuses -- derived from vaccination, previous infection, and hybrid immunity -- on
the clinical outcomes across the Delta and Omicron variants of SARS-CoV-2 \cite{Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI, Levin2021WaningIH}. Utilizing a relatively
diverse dataset of 2,595 healthcare workers in Switzerland, our analysis spread across a timeframe that captured both the Delta and Omicron variant
outbreaks \cite{Heiniger2022DifferencesIC, Diebold2021TemporalTO}.

In aligning with previous studies \cite{Garcia2020ImmuneRI, Papadimitropoulou2017ComparativeEA}, our analysis suggested a trend in the gradient of
symptom severity contingent on the type of immunity. Specifically, we observed that the group with hybrid immunity reported less severe symptoms
compared to those merely vaccinated or those with prior infections but without vaccination \cite{Lipsitch2021SARSCoV2BI}. Most notably, this
protection appeared consistent across both the Delta and Omicron variants, although this observation requires further substantiation due to the
complex and continuously evolving nature of the pandemic \cite{Thakur2022SARSCoV2MA, Azkur2020ImmuneRT}.

While these findings contribute to the understanding of the interplay among immunity statuses, viral variants, and disease severity, several study
limitations warrant mention. The study's observational design inherently carries potential biases. Notably, reliance on symptom-driven testing,
coupled with the study's focus on healthcare workers – who likely have more knowledge about COVID-19 symptoms, may influence the interpretation of the
results. Additionally, while we controlled for several factors such as age, sex, and comorbidities, the potential influence of unmeasured or unknown
factors remains a possibility. Further, the variance in the individual perception and reporting of symptoms highlights the inherent challenge of using
symptom severity as a primary measure of disease severity. Finally, given the ongoing evolution of the pandemic and emergence of new variants, our
dataset may inherently suffer from truncation.

Nonetheless, the trends observed in our study add valuable perspective to the discourse on vaccine strategies and immunity development against
COVID-19. The analysis suggests that hybrid immunity may provide additional protection against severe symptoms of COVID-19, reinforcing the importance
of sustained vaccine coverage among previously infected individuals. At this stage, the observed protective effect of hybrid immunity points to its
potential as a valuable defensive tool against the severity of COVID-19 symptoms, which may have implications for healthcare and policy responses to
the pandemic. However, further investigation in diverse populations and across different settings will be essential to validate and expand upon these
observations. Prospective research may consider evaluating the longevity of this protection and could also explore the role of booster shots in
achieving comparable effects to hybrid immunity.
```
